Australian gunners on a <a href="/wiki/Duckboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Duckboard">duckboard</a> track in Château Wood near <a href="/wiki/Hooge_in_World_War_I" title="Hooge in World War I">Hooge</a>, 29 October 1917. <td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;"><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire"><img alt="British Empire" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> 50 divisions<br />
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/France" title="France"><img alt="France" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/23px-Flag_of_France.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/35px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/45px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> 6 divisions</td>
<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em"><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire"><img alt="German Empire" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Flag_of_the_German_Empire.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_German_Empire.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Flag_of_the_German_Empire.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_German_Empire.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Flag_of_the_German_Empire.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_German_Empire.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a></span> 77–83 divisions</td>

<th colspan="2" style="background-color:#B0C4DE;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;font-size:110%;">Casualties and losses</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width:50%;border-right:1px dotted #aaa;">200,000–448,614<br />
(disputed, see <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Passchendaele#Casualties" title="Battle of Passchendaele">Casualties</a> section)</td>
<td style="width:50%;padding-left:0.25em">217,000–410,000<br />
including 24,065 prisoners (disputed, see <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Passchendaele#Casualties" title="Battle of Passchendaele">Casualties</a> section)</td>
</tr>

<p>The <b>Battle of Passchendaele</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandernschlacht</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>Deuxième Bataille des Flandres</i></span>), also known as the <b>Third Battle of Ypres</b>, was a campaign of the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, fought by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a> against the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> The battle took place on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a>, from July to November 1917, for control of the ridges south and east of the <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Ypres" title="Ypres">Ypres</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Flanders" title="West Flanders">West Flanders</a>, as part of a strategy decided by the Allies at conferences in November 1916 and May 1917. <a href="/wiki/Passendale" title="Passendale">Passchendaele</a> lay on the last ridge east of Ypres, 5&#160;mi (8.0&#160;km) from a railway junction at <a href="/wiki/Roeselare" title="Roeselare">Roulers</a>, which was vital to the supply system of the German <a href="/wiki/4th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="4th Army (German Empire)">4th Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[b]</a></sup> The next stage of the Allied plan was an advance to <a href="/wiki/Torhout" title="Torhout">Thourout</a>–<a href="/wiki/Koekelare" title="Koekelare">Couckelaere</a>, to close the German-controlled railway running through Roulers and Thourout.</p>
<p>Further operations and a British supporting attack along the Belgian coast from Nieuwpoort, combined with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hush" title="Operation Hush">Operation Hush</a> (an amphibious landing), were to have reached <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a> and then the Dutch frontier. The resistance of the 4th Army, unusually wet weather, the onset of winter and the diversion of British and French resources to <a href="/wiki/Italian_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Italian Front (World War I)">Italy</a>, following the Austro-German victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Caporetto" title="Battle of Caporetto">Battle of Caporetto</a> <span class="nowrap">(24 October – 19 November),</span> enabled the Germans to avoid a general withdrawal, which had seemed inevitable in early October. The campaign ended in November, when the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Corps" title="Canadian Corps">Canadian Corps</a> captured Passchendaele, apart from local attacks in December and in early 1918. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Lys_(1918)" title="Battle of the Lys (1918)">Battle of the Lys</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Battle_of_Ypres" title="Fifth Battle of Ypres">Fifth Battle of Ypres</a> were fought before the Allies occupied the Belgian coast and reached the Dutch frontier.</p>
<p>A campaign in Flanders was controversial in 1917 and has remained so. The <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">British Prime Minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, opposed the offensive, as did General <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Foch" title="Ferdinand Foch">Ferdinand Foch</a> the French <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_French_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the French Army">Chief of the General Staff</a>. <a href="/wiki/Field_marshal_(United_Kingdom)" title="Field marshal (United Kingdom)">Field Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Haig,_1st_Earl_Haig" title="Douglas Haig, 1st Earl Haig">Sir Douglas Haig</a>, commanding the <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">British Expeditionary Force</a> (BEF), did not receive approval for the Flanders operation from the War Cabinet until 25 July. Matters of dispute by the participants, writers and historians since the war, have included the wisdom of pursuing an offensive strategy in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Nivelle_Offensive" title="Nivelle Offensive">Nivelle Offensive</a>, rather than waiting for the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces" title="American Expeditionary Forces">American Expeditionary Force</a> (AEF) in France.</p>
<p>The choice of Flanders over areas further south or the Italian front, the climate and weather in Flanders, the choice of <a href="/wiki/General_(United_Kingdom)" title="General (United Kingdom)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Gough" title="Hubert Gough">Hubert Gough</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Fifth Army (United Kingdom)">Fifth Army</a> to conduct the offensive, debates over the nature of the opening attack and between advocates of shallow and deeper objectives, have also been controversial. The passage of time between the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Messines_(1917)" title="Battle of Messines (1917)">Battle of Messines</a> <span class="nowrap">(7–14 June)</span> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pilckem_Ridge" title="Battle of Pilckem Ridge">Battle of Pilckem Ridge</a> <span class="nowrap">(31 July</span>, the opening move of the Third Battle of Ypres), the extent to which the internal troubles of the French armies motivated British persistence with the offensive, the effect of the weather, the decision to continue the offensive in October and the human cost of the campaign on the soldiers of the German and British armies, have also been argued over ever since.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flanders">Flanders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Flanders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1914">1914</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1914">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Belgian_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgian independence">Belgian independence</a> had been recognised in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London</a> (1839), which created a sovereign and neutral state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini1952414_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini1952414-5">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/German_invasion_of_Belgium" title="German invasion of Belgium">German invasion of Belgium</a> on 4 August 1914, in violation of Article VII of the treaty, was the <i><a href="/wiki/Casus_belli" title="Casus belli">casus belli</a></i> of the British government in declaring war on Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini1952504_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini1952504-6">[4]</a></sup> British military operations in Belgium began with the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">British Expeditionary Force</a> (BEF) at <a href="/wiki/Mons" title="Mons">Mons</a> on 22 August. Operations in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a> began during the <a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_Sea" title="Race to the Sea">Race to the Sea</a>, reciprocal attempts by the French and German armies to turn their opponents' northern flank through <a href="/wiki/Picardy" title="Picardy">Picardy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artois" title="Artois">Artois</a> and Flanders. On 10 October, Lieutenant-General <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Falkenhayn" title="Erich von Falkenhayn">Erich von Falkenhayn</a>, the <a href="/wiki/German_General_Staff" title="German General Staff">Chief of Staff</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Oberste_Heeresleitung" title="Oberste Heeresleitung">Oberste Heeresleitung</a></i> (<i>OHL</i>, supreme army command), ordered an attack towards Dunkirk and Calais, followed by a turn south behind the Allied armies, to gain a decisive victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoley2007102_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoley2007102-7">[5]</a></sup> On 16 October, the Belgians and some French reinforcements began the defence of western Belgium and the French <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">Channel</a> ports, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Yser" title="Battle of the Yser">Battle of the Yser</a>. When the German offensive failed, Falkenhayn ordered the capture of <a href="/wiki/Ypres" title="Ypres">Ypres</a> to gain a local advantage. By 18 November, the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Ypres" title="First Battle of Ypres">First Battle of Ypres</a> had also ended in failure, at a cost of <span class="nowrap">160,000 German</span> casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoley2007104_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoley2007104-8">[6]</a></sup> In December 1914, the <a href="/wiki/British_Admiralty" class="mw-redirect" title="British Admiralty">British Admiralty</a> began discussions with the <a href="/wiki/War_Office" title="War Office">War Office</a>, for a combined operation to re-occupy the Belgian coast but the British were obliged to conform to French strategy and participate in offensives further south.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19322_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19322-9">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1915">1915</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1915">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Large British offensive operations in Flanders were not possible in 1915, due to a lack of resources.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoughty2005137_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoughty2005137-10">[8]</a></sup> The Germans conducted their own Flanders offensive at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Ypres" title="Second Battle of Ypres">Second Battle of Ypres</a> (22 April – 15 May 1915), making the Ypres <a href="/wiki/Salients,_re-entrants_and_pockets" class="mw-redirect" title="Salients, re-entrants and pockets">salient</a> more costly to defend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19321_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19321-11">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Douglas_Haig" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Douglas Haig">Sir Douglas Haig</a> succeeded <a href="/wiki/John_French,_1st_Earl_of_Ypres" title="John French, 1st Earl of Ypres">Sir John French</a> as Commander-in-Chief of the BEF on 19 December 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine197712.E2.80.9313_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine197712.E2.80.9313-12">[10]</a></sup> A week after his appointment, Haig met Vice-Admiral Sir <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Bacon" title="Reginald Bacon">Reginald Bacon</a>, who emphasised the importance of obtaining control of the Belgian coast, to end the threat posed by <a href="/wiki/Flanders_U-boat_flotillas" title="Flanders U-boat flotillas">German U-boats</a>. Haig was sceptical of a coastal operation, believing that a landing from the sea would be far more difficult than anticipated and that an advance along the coast would require so much preparation, that the Germans would have ample warning. Haig preferred an advance from Ypres, to bypass the flooded area around the Yser and the coast, before attempting a coastal attack to clear the coast to the Dutch border.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19322_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19322-9">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1916">1916</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1916">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Battle of Verdun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a></div>

<div id="Local_operations.2C_December_1915_.E2.80.93_June_1916" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em">Local operations, December 1915 – June 1916</span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"><b>Actions</b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phosgene_attack_19_December_1915" title="Phosgene attack 19 December 1915">Gas: Wieltje</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Actions_of_the_Bluff,_1916" title="Actions of the Bluff, 1916">The Bluff</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hohenzollern_Redoubt_(1916)" title="Hohenzollern Redoubt (1916)">Hohenzollern Redoubt</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Actions_of_St_Eloi_Craters" class="mw-redirect" title="The Actions of St Eloi Craters">St Eloi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gas_attack_at_Hulluch" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas attack at Hulluch">Gas: Hulluch</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gas_attack_at_Wulverghem" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas attack at Wulverghem">Gas: Wulverghem</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Loss_of_the_Kink_Salient" title="Loss of the Kink Salient">Kink Salient</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_German_Attack_on_Vimy_Ridge,_21_May_1916" title="The German Attack on Vimy Ridge, 21 May 1916">Vimy Ridge</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mont_Sorrel" title="Battle of Mont Sorrel">Mt Sorrel</a></li>
</ul>
<hr />
<p><b>Associated articles</b></p>
<ul>
<li>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ypres_Salient" title="Ypres Salient">Ypres Salient</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hooge_in_World_War_I" title="Hooge in World War I">Hooge in World War I</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
<p>Minor operations took place in the Ypres salient in 1916, some being German initiatives to distract the Allies from the preparations for the offensive at Verdun and later attempts to divert Allied resources from the Battle of the Somme. Other operations were begun by the British to regain territory or to evict the Germans from ground overlooking their positions. Engagements took place on 12 February at Boesinghe and on 14 February at <a href="/wiki/Hooge_in_World_War_I" title="Hooge in World War I">Hooge</a> and Sanctuary Wood. There were actions from <span class="nowrap">14–15 February</span> and <span class="nowrap">1–4 March</span> at <a href="/wiki/The_Bluff,_Ypres" class="mw-redirect" title="The Bluff, Ypres">The Bluff</a>, <span class="nowrap">27 March – 16</span> April at the <a href="/wiki/Sint-Elooi" title="Sint-Elooi">St Eloi Craters</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mont_Sorrel" title="Battle of Mont Sorrel">Battle of Mont Sorrel</a> from <span class="nowrap">2–13 June.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1932163.E2.80.93245_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1932163.E2.80.93245-13">[11]</a></sup> In January 1917, the Second Army (General <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Plumer" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Plumer">Herbert Plumer</a>) with the II Anzac, IX, X and VIII corps, held the Western Front in Flanders from Laventie to Boesinghe, with eleven divisions and up to two in reserve. There was much trench mortaring, mining and raiding by both sides and from January to May, the Second Army had <span class="nowrap">20,000 casualties.</span> In May, reinforcements began arriving to Flanders from the south; the II Corps headquarters and <span class="nowrap">17 divisions</span> had arrived by the end of the month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalls1940533.E2.80.93534_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalls1940533.E2.80.93534-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 1916, Plumer began to plan offensives against <a href="/wiki/Messines_Ridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Messines Ridge">Messines Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lille" title="Lille">Lille</a> and Houthulst Forest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds193231_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds193231-15">[13]</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Rawlinson,_1st_Baron_Rawlinson" title="Henry Rawlinson, 1st Baron Rawlinson">Henry Rawlinson</a> was also ordered to plan an attack from the Ypres Salient on 4 February; planning continued but the Battle of Verdun and the Battle of the Somme took up the rest of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine197714.E2.80.9315_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine197714.E2.80.9315-16">[14]</a></sup> In November 1916, Haig, the French commander-in-chief <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joffre" title="Joseph Joffre">Joseph Joffre</a> and the other Allies met at <a href="/wiki/Chantilly,_Oise" title="Chantilly, Oise">Chantilly</a>. The commanders agreed on a strategy of simultaneous attacks, to overwhelm the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_of_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Front of World War I">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_I)" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian Campaign (World War I)">Italian</a> fronts, by the first fortnight of February 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartSteel200130_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartSteel200130-17">[15]</a></sup> A meeting in London of the Admiralty and the General Staff urged that the Flanders operation be undertaken in 1917 and Joffre replied on 8 December, agreeing to a Flanders campaign after the spring offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalls194021_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalls194021-18">[16]</a></sup> The plan for a year of <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attrition</a> offensives on the Western Front, with the main effort to be made in the summer by the BEF, was scrapped by the new French Commander-in-Chief <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nivelle" title="Robert Nivelle">Robert Nivelle</a> in favour of a return to a strategy of decisive battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalls194038.E2.80.9339_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalls194038.E2.80.9339-19">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allied_strategy">Allied strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Allied strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front (World War I)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nivelle_Offensive" title="Nivelle Offensive">Nivelle Offensive</a></div>
<p>Nivelle planned an operation in three parts, with preliminary offensives to pin German reserves by the British at <a href="/wiki/Arras" title="Arras">Arras</a> and the French between the Somme and the <a href="/wiki/Oise" title="Oise">Oise</a>, then a French breakthrough offensive on the <a href="/wiki/Aisne" title="Aisne">Aisne</a>, followed by pursuit and exploitation. The plan was welcomed by Haig but with some reservations, which he addressed on 6 January. Nivelle agreed to a proviso that if the first two parts of the operation failed to lead to a breakthrough, the operations would be stopped so that the British could move their forces north for the Flanders offensive, which Haig stressed was of great importance to the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmondsWynne194014_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmondsWynne194014-20">[18]</a></sup> On 23 January, Haig wrote that it would take six weeks to move British troops and equipment from the Arras front to Flanders and on 14 March, he noted that the attack on Messines Ridge could be made in May. On 21 March, he wrote to Nivelle that it would take two months to prepare the attacks from Messines to Steenstraat but that the Messines attack could be ready in <span class="nowrap">5–6 weeks.</span> The Nivelle Offensive took place from 9 April to 9 May and failed to achieve a breakthrough. On 16 May, Haig wrote that he had divided the Flanders operation into two phases, one to take Messines Ridge and the main attack several weeks later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine197731.2C_55.2C_94_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine197731.2C_55.2C_94-21">[19]</a></sup> British determination to clear the Belgian coast took on more urgency, after the Germans resumed unrestricted submarine warfare on 1 February 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine199915_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine199915-22">[20]</a></sup> On 1 May 1917, Haig wrote that the Nivelle Offensive had weakened the German army but that an attempt at a decisive blow would be premature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell1993169_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell1993169-23">[21]</a></sup> An offensive at Ypres would continue the wearing-out process, on a front where the Germans could not refuse to fight. Even a partial success would improve the tactical situation in the Ypres salient, reducing wastage, which was exceptional, even in quiet periods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine197784_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine197784-24">[22]</a></sup> In early May, Haig set the timetable for the Flanders offensive, with the preliminary attack on Messines Ridge to begin on 7 June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199124_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199124-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kerensky_offensive">Kerensky offensive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Kerensky offensive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern Front (World War I)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerensky_Offensive" title="Kerensky Offensive">Kerensky Offensive</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EasternFront1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/EasternFront1917.jpg/170px-EasternFront1917.jpg" width="170" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/EasternFront1917.jpg/255px-EasternFront1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/EasternFront1917.jpg/340px-EasternFront1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="948" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EasternFront1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">The Eastern Front in 1917</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Russian army conducted the Kerensky Offensive in <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Eastern_Europe)" title="Galicia (Eastern Europe)">Galicia</a>, to honour the agreement struck with the Allies at the Chantilly meeting of 15 to 16 November 1916. After a brief period of success from 1 to 19 July, the Russian offensive was contained by the German and Austro-Hungarian armies, which counter-attacked and forced the Russian armies to retreat. On the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a> coast from 1 to 5 September 1917, the Germans attacked with their strategic reserve of six divisions and captured <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Operation_Albion" title="Operation Albion">Operation Albion</a> (September–October 1917), the Germans took the <a href="/wiki/West_Estonian_Archipelago" class="mw-redirect" title="West Estonian Archipelago">islands</a> at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Riga" title="Gulf of Riga">Gulf of Riga</a>. The British and French commanders on the Western Front had to reckon on the German western army <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">(<i>Westheer</i>)</span> being strengthened by reinforcements from the <i>Ostheer</i> on the Eastern Front by late 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991234_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991234-26">[24]</a></sup> Haig wished to exploit the diversion of German forces in Russia for as long as it continued and urged the British War Cabinet to commit the maximum amount of manpower and munitions to the battle in Flanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977290.E2.80.93297_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977290.E2.80.93297-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prelude">Prelude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Prelude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ypres_salient">Ypres salient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ypres salient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ypres is overlooked by <a href="/wiki/Kemmelberg" title="Kemmelberg">Kemmel Hill</a> in the south-west and from the east by a line of low hills running south-west to north-east Wytschaete (<a href="/wiki/Wijtschate" class="mw-redirect" title="Wijtschate">Wijtschate</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Hill_60_(Ypres)" title="Hill 60 (Ypres)">Hill 60</a> are to the east of Verbrandenmolen, <a href="/wiki/Hooge,_Ypres" title="Hooge, Ypres">Hooge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polygon_Wood,_Zonnebeke" title="Polygon Wood, Zonnebeke">Polygon Wood</a> and Passchendaele (<a href="/wiki/Passendale" title="Passendale">Passendale</a>). The high point of the ridge is at Wytschaete, 7,000&#160;yd (6,400&#160;m) from Ypres, while at <a href="/wiki/Hollebeke" title="Hollebeke">Hollebeke</a> the ridge is 4,000&#160;yd (3,700&#160;m) distant and recedes to 7,000&#160;yd (6,400&#160;m) at Polygon Wood. Wytschaete is about 150&#160;ft (46&#160;m) above the plain; on the Ypres–Menin road at Hooge, the elevation is about 100&#160;ft (30&#160;m) and 70&#160;ft (21&#160;m) at Passchendaele. The rises are slight, apart from the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Zonnebeke" title="Zonnebeke">Zonnebeke</a>, which has a gradient of <span class="nowrap">1:33.</span> From Hooge and further east, the slope is <span class="nowrap">1:60 and</span> near Hollebeke, it is <span class="nowrap">1:75; the</span> heights are subtle and resemble a saucer lip around the city. The main ridge has spurs sloping east and one is particularly noticeable at Wytschaete, which runs 2&#160;mi (3.2&#160;km) south-east to Messines (<a href="/wiki/Mesen" title="Mesen">Mesen</a>) with a gentle slope on the east side and a <span class="nowrap">1:10 decline</span> westwards. Further south, is the muddy valley of the Douve river, <a href="/wiki/Ploegsteert_Wood" title="Ploegsteert Wood">Ploegsteert Wood</a> (Plugstreet to the British) and Hill 63. West of Messines Ridge is the parallel Wulverghem (<a href="/wiki/Spanbroekmolen" title="Spanbroekmolen">Spanbroekmolen</a>) Spur and on the east side, the Oosttaverne Spur, which is also parallel to the main ridge. The general aspect south and east of Ypres, is one of low ridges and dips, gradually flattening northwards beyond Passchendaele, into a featureless plain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1925128.E2.80.93129_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1925128.E2.80.93129-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Possession of the higher ground to the south and east of Ypres, gives an army ample scope for ground observation, <a href="/wiki/Enfilade_and_defilade" title="Enfilade and defilade">enfilade fire</a> and converging artillery bombardments. An occupier also has the advantage that artillery deployments and the movement of reinforcements, supplies and stores can be screened from view. The ridge had woods from Wytschaete to Zonnebeke giving good cover, some being of notable size, like Polygon Wood and those later named Battle Wood, Shrewsbury Forest and <a href="/wiki/Sanctuary_Wood" title="Sanctuary Wood">Sanctuary Wood</a>. In 1914, the woods usually had undergrowth but by 1917, artillery bombardments had reduced the woods to tree stumps, shattered tree trunks tangled with barbed wire and more wire festooning the ground, which was full of shell-holes; fields in the gaps between the woods, were 800–1,000&#160;yd (730–910&#160;m) wide and devoid of cover. The main road to Ypres from <a href="/wiki/Poperinge" title="Poperinge">Poperinge</a> to <a href="/wiki/Vlamertinge" class="mw-redirect" title="Vlamertinge">Vlamertinge</a> is in a defile, easily observed from the ridge. A century ago, roads in the area were unpaved, except for the main ones from Ypres, with occasional villages and houses dotted along them. The lowland west of the ridge, was a mixture of meadow and fields, with high hedgerows dotted with trees, cut by streams and a network of drainage ditches emptying into canals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1925129.E2.80.93131_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1925129.E2.80.93131-29">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg/220px-USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg" width="220" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg/330px-USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg/440px-USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1938" data-file-height="2992" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USMA_-_Third_Battle_of_Ypres.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">The progression of the battle and the general disposition of troops</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Flanders, sands, gravels and <a href="/wiki/Marl" title="Marl">marls</a> predominate, covered by <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silts</a> in places. The coastal strip is sandy but a short way into the hinterland, the ground rises towards the vale of Ypres, which before 1914 was a flourishing market garden.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle1997140.E2.80.93158_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle1997140.E2.80.93158-30">[28]</a></sup> Ypres is 66&#160;ft (20&#160;m) above sea level; Bixschoote 4&#160;mi (6.4&#160;km) to the north is at 28&#160;ft (8.5&#160;m). To the east the land is at 66–82&#160;ft (20–25&#160;m) for several miles, with the Steenbeek river at 49&#160;ft (15&#160;m) near St Julien. There is a low ridge from Messines, 260&#160;ft (80&#160;m) at its highest point, running north-east past Clapham Junction at the west end of Gheluvelt plateau (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">2<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> miles from Ypres at 213&#160;ft (65&#160;m) and Gheluvelt, above 160&#160;ft (50&#160;m) to Passchendaele, (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">5<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> miles from Ypres at 160&#160;ft (50&#160;m) declining from there to a plain further north. Gradients vary from negligible, to <span class="nowrap">1:60</span> at Hooge and <span class="nowrap">1:33</span> at Zonnebeke.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle1997141_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle1997141-31">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Underneath the soil is <a href="/wiki/London_clay" class="mw-redirect" title="London clay">London clay</a>, sand and silt; according to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_War_Graves_Commission" title="Commonwealth War Graves Commission">Commonwealth War Graves Commission</a> categories of <i>sand</i>, <i>sandy soils</i> and <i>well-balanced soils</i>, Messines ridge is well-balanced soil and the ground around Ypres is sandy soil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle1997142_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle1997142-32">[30]</a></sup> The ground is drained by many streams, canals and ditches, which need regular maintenance. Since 1914 much of the drainage had been destroyed, though some parts were restored by land drainage companies from England. The British considered the area drier than <a href="/wiki/Loos-en-Gohelle" title="Loos-en-Gohelle">Loos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Givenchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Givenchy">Givenchy</a> and Plugstreet Wood further south.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991125_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991125-33">[31]</a></sup> A 1989 study of weather data recorded at Lille, 16&#160;mi (26&#160;km) from Ypres from <span class="nowrap">1867–1916,</span> showed that August was more often dry than wet, that there was a trend towards dry autumns (September–November) and that average rainfall in October had decreased over the previous fifty years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle1997147.E2.80.93148_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle1997147.E2.80.93148-34">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_plans">British plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: British plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Preparations for operations in Flanders began in 1915, with the doubling of the Hazebrouck–Ypres rail line and the building of a new line from Bergues to Proven, which was doubled in early 1917. Progress on roads, rail lines, railheads and spurs in the Second Army zone was continuous and by mid-1917, gave the area the most efficient supply system of the BEF.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenniker1937273_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenniker1937273-35">[33]</a></sup> Several plans and memoranda for a Flanders offensive were produced between January 1916 and May 1917, in which the writers tried to relate the offensive resources available to the terrain and the likely German defence. In early 1916, the importance of the capture of the Gheluvelt plateau for an advance further north was emphasised by Haig and the army commanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19913.E2.80.934_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19913.E2.80.934-36">[34]</a></sup> On 14 February 1917, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Norman_MacMullen" title="Norman MacMullen">C. N. Macmullen</a> of GHQ proposed that the plateau be taken by a mass tank attack, reducing the need for artillery; in April a reconnaissance by Captain <a href="/wiki/Giffard_LeQuesne_Martel" class="mw-redirect" title="Giffard LeQuesne Martel">G. le Q Martel</a> found that the area was unsuitable for tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199125_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199125-37">[35]</a></sup> On 9 February, General Rawlinson, commander of the Fourth Army, suggested that Messines Ridge could be taken in one day and that the capture of the Gheluvelt plateau should be fundamental to the attack further north. He suggested that the southern attack from St Yves to Mont Sorrel should come first and that Mont Sorrel to Steenstraat should be attacked within <span class="nowrap">48–72 hours.</span> After discussions with Rawlinson and Plumer and the incorporation of Haig's changes, Macmullen submitted his memorandum on 14 February. With amendments the memorandum became the <i>GHQ 1917</i> plan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199117.E2.80.9319_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199117.E2.80.9319-38">[36]</a></sup> A week after the Battle of Messines Ridge, Haig gave his objectives to his army commanders, wearing out the enemy, securing the Belgian coast and connecting with the Dutch frontier by capturing Passchendaele ridge, followed by an advance on Roulers and Operation Hush, an attack along the coast with a combined amphibious landing. If manpower and artillery were insufficient, only the first part of the plan might be fulfilled. On 30 April, Haig told Gough, the Fifth Army commander, that he would lead the Northern Operation and the coastal force, although Cabinet approval for the offensive was not granted until 21 June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield2011227.E2.80.93231_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield2011227.E2.80.93231-39">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[c]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="German_defences">German defences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: German defences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_tactics,_1917#German_defensive_preparations:_June.E2.80.93July_1917" title="Western Front tactics, 1917">German defensive preparations: June – July 1917</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg/220px-German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg/330px-German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg/440px-German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="907" data-file-height="498" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:German_defences_east_of_Ypres_mid-1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The British front line and the German defences in the area east of Ypres, mid-1917</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 4th Army held a front of 25&#160;mi (40&#160;km) with three <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Gruppen</i>,</span> composed of a corps headquarters and a varying complement of divisions; Group Staden, based on the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/Guards_Reserve_Corps" title="Guards Reserve Corps">Guards Reserve Corps</a> was added later. Group Dixmude held 12&#160;mi (19&#160;km) with four front divisions and two <a href="/wiki/Eingreif_division" title="Eingreif division"><i>Eingreif</i></a> divisions, Group Ypres held 6&#160;mi (9.7&#160;km) from Pilckem to Menin Road with three front divisions and two <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions and Group <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wijtschate</i></span> held a similar length of front south of the Menin road, with three front divisions and three <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions. The <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions were stationed behind the Menin and Passchendaele ridges. About 5&#160;mi (8.0&#160;km) further back, were four more <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions and 7&#160;mi (11&#160;km) beyond them, another two in <i>OHL</i> reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939297.E2.80.93298_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939297.E2.80.93298-43">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Germans were anxious that the British would attempt to exploit the victory of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Messines_(1917)" title="Battle of Messines (1917)">Battle of Messines</a>, with an advance to the Tower Hamlets spur beyond the north end of Messines Ridge. On 9 June, <a href="/wiki/Rupprecht,_Crown_Prince_of_Bavaria" title="Rupprecht, Crown Prince of Bavaria">Crown Prince Rupprecht</a> proposed a withdrawal to the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern</i></span> line east of Messines. Construction of defences began but was terminated after <a href="/wiki/Fritz_von_Lo%C3%9Fberg" title="Fritz von Loßberg">Fritz von Loßberg</a> was appointed Chief of Staff of the 4th Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939282.E2.80.93283_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939282.E2.80.93283-44">[41]</a></sup> Loßberg rejected the proposed withdrawal to the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern</i></span> line and ordered that the front line east of the <i>Oosttaverne</i> line be held rigidly. The <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern Stellung</i></span> (Flanders Position) along Passchendaele Ridge, in front of the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern</i></span> line, would become <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern I Stellung</i></span> and a new position, <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern II Stellung</i></span>, would run west of Menin, northwards to Passchendaele. Construction of a <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern III Stellung</i></span> east of Menin northwards to Moorslede was also begun. From July 1917, the area east of Ypres was defended by the front position, the <i>Albrecht Stellung</i> (second position), <i>Wilhelm Stellung</i> (third position), <i>Flandern I Stellung</i> (fourth position), <i>Flandern II Stellung</i> (fifth position) and <i>Flandern III Stellung</i>, the sixth position (incomplete). Between the German defences lay villages such as Zonnebeke and Passchendaele, which were fortified and prepared for all-round defence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939284_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939284-45">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 June, <a href="/wiki/Erich_Ludendorff" title="Erich Ludendorff">Erich Ludendorff</a>, the First Quartermaster General, suggested to Crown Prince Rupprecht that Group Ypres should withdraw to the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wilhelm Stellung</i></span>, leaving only outposts in the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Albrecht Stellung</i></span>. On 30 June, the army group Chief of Staff, <a href="/wiki/General_von_Kuhl" class="mw-redirect" title="General von Kuhl">General von Kuhl</a>, suggested a withdrawal to the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern I Stellung</i></span> along Passchendaele ridge, meeting the old front line in the north near Langemarck and Armentières in the south. Such a withdrawal would avoid a hasty retreat from Pilckem Ridge and force the British into a time-consuming redeployment. Lossberg disagreed, believing that the British would launch a broad front offensive, that the ground east of the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Sehnen</i></span> line was easy to defend and that the Menin road ridge could be held if it was made the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Schwerpunkt</i></span> (point of main effort) of the German defensive system. Pilckem Ridge deprived the British of ground observation over the Steenbeek Valley, while the Germans could see the area from Passchendaele Ridge, allowing German infantry to be supported by observed artillery-fire. Lossberg's judgement was accepted and no withdrawal was made.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939286.E2.80.93287_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939286.E2.80.93287-46">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Messines">Battle of Messines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Battle of Messines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Messines_(1917)" title="Battle of Messines (1917)">Battle of Messines (1917)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg/220px-NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg/330px-NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg/440px-NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2407" data-file-height="1619" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NLS_Haig_-_Smashed_up_German_trench_on_Messines_Ridge_with_dead.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">German trench destroyed by a mine explosion</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first stage in the British plan was a preparatory attack on the German positions south of Ypres at Messines Ridge. The Germans on the ridge had observation over Ypres and unless it was captured, observed <a href="/wiki/Enfilade" class="mw-redirect" title="Enfilade">enfilade</a> artillery-fire could be fired against a British attack from the salient further north.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon20071_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon20071-47">[44]</a></sup> Since mid-1915, the British had been <a href="/wiki/Mining_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining (military)">mining</a> under the German positions on the ridge and by June 1917, <a href="/wiki/Mines_in_the_Battle_of_Messines_(1917)" title="Mines in the Battle of Messines (1917)">21 mines</a> had been filled with nearly 1,000,000&#160;lb (450&#160;t) of explosives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartSteel200141.E2.80.9344_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartSteel200141.E2.80.9344-48">[45]</a></sup> The Germans knew the British were mining and had taken counter-measures but they were surprised at the extent of the British effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon200723_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon200723-49">[46]</a></sup> Two of the mines failed to detonate but <span class="nowrap">19 went</span> off on 7 June, at <span class="nowrap">3:10 a.m.</span> <a href="/wiki/British_Summer_Time" title="British Summer Time">British Summer Time</a>. The final objectives were largely gained before dark and the British had fewer losses than the expected <span class="nowrap">50 percent in</span> the initial attack. As the infantry advanced over the far edge of the ridge, German artillery and machine-guns east of the ridge opened fire and the British artillery was less able to suppress them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartSteel200155_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartSteel200155-50">[47]</a></sup> The attack removed the Germans from the dominating ground on the southern face of the Ypres salient, which the 4th Army had held since the First Battle of Ypres in 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199187_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199187-51">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battles">Battles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Battles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="July–August"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="July.E2.80.93August">July–August</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: July–August">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_tactics,_1917#The_British_set-piece_attack_in_mid-1917" title="Western Front tactics, 1917">The British set-piece attack in mid-1917</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg/220px-18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg/330px-18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg/440px-18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="636" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:18pdrBoesinghe31July1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">British <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_QF_18_pounder" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordnance QF 18 pounder">18 pounder</a> battery taking up new positions near Boesinghe, 31 July</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Haig selected Gough to command the offensive on 30 April and on 10 June, Gough took over the Ypres salient north of Messines Ridge. Gough planned an offensive based on the <i>GHQ 1917</i> plan and the instructions he had received from Haig.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991127_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991127-52">[49]</a></sup> Gough held meetings with his corps commanders on 6 and 16 June, where the third objective, which included the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wilhelm Stellung</i></span> (third line), a second-day objective in earlier plans, was added to the two objectives due to be taken on the first day. A fourth objective was also given for the first day but was only to be attempted at the discretion of divisional and corps commanders, in places where the German defence had collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991126.E2.80.93127.2C_431.E2.80.93432_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991126.E2.80.93127.2C_431.E2.80.93432-53">[50]</a></sup> The attack was not planned as a breakthrough operation, because <i>Flandern I Stellung</i>, the fourth German defensive position, lay 10,000–12,000&#160;yd (9,100–11,000&#160;m) behind the front line and was not an objective on the first day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199672.E2.80.9375_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199672.E2.80.9375-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Fifth Army plan was more ambitious than the plans devised by Rawlinson and Plumer, which had involved an advance of 1,000–1,750&#160;yd (910–1,600&#160;m) on the first day, by compressing their first three attacks into one day instead of three.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199672.E2.80.9375_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199672.E2.80.9375-54">[51]</a></sup> Major-General <a href="/wiki/John_Humphrey_Davidson" title="John Humphrey Davidson">John Davidson</a>, Director of Operations at GHQ, wrote in a memorandum that there was "ambiguity as to what was meant by a step-by-step attack with limited objectives" and suggested reverting to a 1,750&#160;yd (1,600&#160;m) advance on the first day to increase the concentration of British artillery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson195329_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson195329-55">[52]</a></sup> Gough stressed the need to plan to exploit an opportunity to take ground left temporarily undefended, which was more likely in the first attack, which would have the benefit of long preparation. This had not been done in earlier battles and vacant ground, there for the taking, had been re-occupied by the Germans. At the end of June, Haig added a division to II Corps (Lieutenant-General <a href="/wiki/Claud_Jacob" title="Claud Jacob">Claud Jacob</a>) from the Second Army and next day, after meeting with Gough and General <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Plumer,_1st_Viscount_Plumer" title="Herbert Plumer, 1st Viscount Plumer">Herbert Plumer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Second Army (United Kingdom)">Second Army</a> commander, Haig endorsed the Fifth Army plan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991440_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991440-56">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Pilckem_Ridge">Battle of Pilckem Ridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Battle of Pilckem Ridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pilckem_Ridge" title="Battle of Pilckem Ridge">Battle of Pilckem Ridge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Passchendaele,_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg/220px-The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg/330px-The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg/440px-The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="637" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Passchendaele,_July-november_1917_Q5726.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">German prisoners and British wounded cross the Yser Canal near Boesinghe, 31 July 1917. (Q5726)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The British attack began at <span class="nowrap">3:50 a.m.</span> on 31 July; the attack was to commence at dawn but a layer of unbroken low cloud, meant that it was still dark when the infantry advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199689_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199689-57">[54]</a></sup> The main attack, by <a href="/wiki/II_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="II Corps (United Kingdom)">II Corps</a> across the Ghelveult Plateau to the south, confronted the principal German defensive concentration of artillery, ground-holding divisions (<i>Stellungsdivisionen</i>) and <i>Eingreif</i> divisions. The attack had most success on the northern flank, on the fronts of <a href="/wiki/XIV_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="XIV Corps (United Kingdom)">XIV Corps</a> and the French First Army, both of which advanced 2,500–3,000&#160;yd (2,300–2,700&#160;m) to the line of the Steenbeek river. In the centre, <a href="/wiki/XVIII_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="XVIII Corps (United Kingdom)">XVIII Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/XIX_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="XIX Corps (United Kingdom)">XIX Corps</a> pushed forward to the line of the Steenbeek (black line) to consolidate and sent fresh troops towards the green line and on the XIX Corps front to the red line, for an advance of about 4,000&#160;yd (3,700&#160;m). Group Ypres counter-attacked the flanks of the British break-in, supported by every artillery piece and aircraft, around noon. The Germans were able to drive the three British brigades back to the black line with <span class="nowrap">70 percent</span> casualties; the counter-attack was stopped at the black line by mud, artillery and machine-gun fire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199690.E2.80.9395_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson199690.E2.80.9395-58">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Capture_of_Westhoek">Capture of Westhoek</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Capture of Westhoek">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Westhoek" title="Capture of Westhoek">Capture of Westhoek</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:German_defensive_system,_Flanders,_mid-1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/German_defensive_system%2C_Flanders%2C_mid-1917.jpg/220px-German_defensive_system%2C_Flanders%2C_mid-1917.jpg" width="220" height="395" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/German_defensive_system%2C_Flanders%2C_mid-1917.jpg/330px-German_defensive_system%2C_Flanders%2C_mid-1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bc/German_defensive_system%2C_Flanders%2C_mid-1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:German_defensive_system,_Flanders,_mid-1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">German defensive system, Flanders, mid-1917</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After a rain delay from 2 August, II Corps attacked again on 10 August, to capture the rest of the black line (second objective) on the Gheluvelt plateau. The advance succeeded but German artillery-fire and infantry counter-attacks isolated the infantry of the 18th Division in Glencorse Wood. At about <span class="nowrap">7:00 p.m.,</span> German infantry attacked behind a smokescreen and recaptured all but the north-west corner of the wood; only the 25th Division gains on Westhoek Ridge to the north were held.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991185.E2.80.93187_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991185.E2.80.93187-59">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_von_Thaer" title="Albrecht von Thaer">Albrecht von Thaer</a>, a staff officer at Group Wytshchate, noted that casualties after <span class="nowrap">14 days</span> in the line averaged <span class="nowrap">1,500–2,000 men,</span> compared to the Somme 1916 average of <span class="nowrap">4,000 men</span> and that German troop morale was higher than in 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358-60">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Hill_70">Battle of Hill 70</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Battle of Hill 70">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Oppy_Wood" title="Capture of Oppy Wood">Capture of Oppy Wood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hill_70" title="Battle of Hill 70">Battle of Hill 70</a></div>
<p>Attacks to threaten Lens and Lille were to be made by the <a href="/wiki/First_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="First Army (United Kingdom)">First Army</a> in late June near Gavrelle and Oppy, along the Souchez river. The objective was to eliminate a German salient between Avion and the west end of <a href="/wiki/Lens,_Pas-de-Calais" title="Lens, Pas-de-Calais">Lens</a>, by taking reservoir Hill (Hill 65) and Hill 70. The attacks were conducted earlier than planned to use heavy and siege artillery before it was transferred to Ypres, the Souchez operation being cut back and the attack on Hill 70 postponed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991112.E2.80.93113_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991112.E2.80.93113-61">[58]</a></sup> The Battle of Hill 70, 30&#160;mi (48&#160;km) south of Ypres, eventually took place from 15 to 25 August The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Corps" title="Canadian Corps">Canadian Corps</a> fought five divisions of the German <a href="/wiki/6th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="6th Army (German Empire)">6th Army</a> in the operation that had been postponed from July. The capture of Hill 70 was a costly success in which three Canadian divisions inflicted many casualties on the German divisions opposite and pinned down troops reserved for the relief of tired divisions in Flanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991219.E2.80.93230_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991219.E2.80.93230-62">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Kuhl" title="Hermann von Kuhl">Hermann von Kuhl</a>, chief of staff of Army Group Crown Prince Rupprecht, wrote later that it was a costly defeat and wrecked the plan for relieving fought-out (exhausted) divisions in Flanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977234_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977234-63">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Langemarck">Battle of Langemarck</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Battle of Langemarck">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Langemarck_(1917)" title="Battle of Langemarck (1917)">Battle of Langemarck (1917)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hush" title="Operation Hush">Operation Hush</a></div>
<p>The Battle of Langemarck was fought from <span class="nowrap">16–18 August;</span> the Fifth Army headquarters was influenced by the effect that delay would have on Operation Hush, which needed the high tides due at the end of August or it would have to be postponed for a month. Gough intended that the rest of the green line, just beyond the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wilhelm Stellung</i></span> (German third line), from Polygon Wood to Langemarck, was to be captured and the Steenbeek crossed further north.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991189.E2.80.93202_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991189.E2.80.93202-64">[61]</a></sup> In the II Corps area, the disappointment of 10 August was repeated, with the infantry managing to advance, then being isolated by German artillery and forced back to their start line by German counter-attacks, except in the 25th Division area near Westhoek. Attempts by the German infantry to advance further were stopped by British artillery-fire with many losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991194_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991194-65">[62]</a></sup> The advance further north in the XVIII Corps area retook and held the north end of St Julien and the area south-east of Langemarck, while XIV Corps captured Langemarck and the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wilhelm Stellung</i></span>, north of the Ypres–Staden railway near the Kortebeek stream. The French First Army conformed, pushing up to the Kortebeek and St Jansbeck stream west of the northern stretch of the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wilhelm Stellung</i></span>, where it crossed to the east side of the Kortebeek.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991201_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991201-66">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_attacks">Local attacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Local attacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Operations_on_the_Gheluvelt_Plateau,_July%E2%80%93August_1917" title="Operations on the Gheluvelt Plateau, July–August 1917">Gheluvelt Plateau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Action_of_the_Cockcroft" class="mw-redirect" title="Action of the Cockcroft">Action of the Cockcroft</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Action_of_22_August_1917" title="Action of 22 August 1917">Action of 22 August 1917</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg/220px-AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg/330px-AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="434" data-file-height="313" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AAGunMorbecque29August1917-2.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">British anti-aircraft gun at Morbecque, 29 August 1917</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On higher ground, the Germans continued to inflict many losses on the British divisions beyond Langemarck but on 19 August, after two fine dry days, XVIII Corps conducted a novel infantry, tank, aircraft and artillery operation and captured German strongpoints and pillboxes along the St Julien–Poelcappelle road in front of the <i>Wilhelm Stellung</i>. On 22 August, more ground was gained by XIX and XVIII corps but they remained overlooked by the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991203_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991203-67">[64]</a></sup> A II Corps attack on the Gheluvelt Plateau from 22 to 24 August, to capture Nonne Bosschen, Glencorse Wood and Inverness Copse, failed and was costly to both sides.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991202.E2.80.93205_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991202.E2.80.93205-68">[65]</a></sup> Gough laid down a new infantry formation of skirmish lines to be followed by "worms" on 24 August and Cavan noted that pillboxes should be attacked on a broad front, to engage them simultaneously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001130.E2.80.93134_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001130.E2.80.93134-69">[66]</a></sup> Another general offensive intended for 25 August, was delayed by the failure of the preliminary attacks and then postponed due to more bad weather.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers2010162.E2.80.93167_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers2010162.E2.80.93167-70">[67]</a></sup> On 27 August, II Corps tried a combined tank and infantry attack but the tanks bogged and the attack failed; Haig called a halt to operations until the weather improved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991208_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991208-71">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Weather">Weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Weather">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <i>Field Marshal Earl Haig</i> (1929), Brigadier-General <a href="/wiki/John_Charteris" title="John Charteris">John Charteris</a>, the BEF Chief of Intelligence from 1915 to 1918, wrote that</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Careful investigation of records of more than eighty years showed that in Flanders the weather broke early each August with the regularity of the Indian monsoon: once the Autumn rains set in difficulties would be greatly enhanced....Unfortunately, there now set in the wettest August for thirty years.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Charteris<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharteris1929272.E2.80.93273_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharteris1929272.E2.80.93273-72">[69]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>the first part of which was quoted by Lloyd George (1934), Liddell Hart (1934) and Leon Wolff (1959); in a 1997 essay, John Hussey called the passage by Charteris "baffling".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussey1997155_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussey1997155-73">[70]</a></sup> The BEF had set up a Meteorological Section under <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Gold_(meteorologist)" title="Ernest Gold (meteorologist)">Ernest Gold</a> in 1915, which by the end of 1917 had <span class="nowrap">16 officers</span> and <span class="nowrap">82 men.</span> The section predicted the warm weather and thunderstorms of 7 to 14 June and in a letter to the press of 17 January 1958, Gold wrote that the facts of Flanders climate contradicted the claim made by Charteris in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussey1997153_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussey1997153-74">[71]</a></sup> In 1989, Philip Griffiths examined August weather in Flanders for the thirty years before 1916 and found that,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...there is no reason to suggest that the weather broke early in the month with any regularity.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Griffiths<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussey1997147.E2.80.93148_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussey1997147.E2.80.93148-75">[72]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>From 1901 to 1916, records from a weather station at <a href="/wiki/Cap_Gris_Nez" title="Cap Gris Nez">Cap Gris Nez</a> showed that <span class="nowrap">65 percent</span> of August days were dry and that from 1913 to 1916, there were <span class="nowrap">26, 23, 23 and 21</span> rainless days and monthly rainfall of 17,&#160;28,&#160;22 and 96&#160;mm (0.67,&#160;1.10,&#160;0.87 and 3.78&#160;in);</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...during the summers preceding the Flanders campaign August days were more often dry than wet.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Griffith<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussey1997148_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussey1997148-76">[73]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In August 1917, 127&#160;mm (5.0&#160;in), 84&#160;mm (3.3&#160;in) of rain falling on <span class="nowrap">1, 8, 14, 26 and</span> <span class="nowrap">27 August;</span> the weather was also overcast and windless, which much reduced evaporation. Divided into two ten-day and an eleven-day period, there were 53.6,&#160;32.4 and 41.3&#160;mm (2.11,&#160;1.28 and 1.63&#160;in) of rain that August In the <span class="nowrap">61 hours</span> before <span class="nowrap">6:00 p.m.</span> on 31 July, 12.5&#160;mm (0.49&#160;in) of rain fell and from <span class="nowrap">6:00 p.m.</span> on 31 July to <span class="nowrap">6:00 p.m.</span> on 4 August, there was 63&#160;mm (2.5&#160;in) of rain. There were three dry days and <span class="nowrap">14 days</span> with less than 1&#160;mm (0.039&#160;in) of rain during the month. Three days were sunless and one had six minutes of sun; over <span class="nowrap">27 days</span> there were <span class="nowrap">178.1 hours</span> of sunshine, an average of <span class="nowrap">6.6 hours</span> per day. Hussey wrote that the weather in August 1917 was exceptionally bad and Haig had been justified in expecting little rain, which would be dried by sunshine and breezes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussey1997149.E2.80.93151_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussey1997149.E2.80.93151-77">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Verdun">Verdun</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Verdun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun#French_counter-offensives_in_late_1916_and_August_1917" title="Battle of Verdun">Verdun, August 1917</a></div>
<p>Petain had committed the French Second Army to an attack at Verdun in mid-July, in support of the operations in Flanders. The attack was delayed, partly due to mutinies in the French army after the failure of the <a href="/wiki/Nivelle_Offensive" title="Nivelle Offensive">Nivelle Offensive</a>, and also because of a German attack at Verdun from <span class="nowrap">28–29 June,</span> which captured some of the ground intended as a jumping-off point for the French attack. A French counter-attack on 17 July re-captured the ground, the Germans regained it on 1 August, then took ground on the east bank on 16 August.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoughty2005380.E2.80.93383_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoughty2005380.E2.80.93383-78">[75]</a></sup> The battle began on 20 August and by 9 September, the French had taken <span class="nowrap">10,000 prisoners.</span> Fighting continued sporadically into October, adding to the German difficulties on the Western Front and elsewhere. Ludendorff wrote</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>On the left bank, close to the Meuse, one division had failed&#160;... and yet both here and in Flanders everything possible had been done to avoid failure&#160;... The French army was once more capable of the offensive. It had quickly overcome its depression.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Ludendorff: <i>Memoirs</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977235_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977235-79">[76]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>yet no German counter-attack was possible, because the local <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions had been transferred to Flanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991230_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991230-80">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="September–October"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="September.E2.80.93October">September–October</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: September–October">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_tactics,_1917#The_British_set-piece_attack_in_late_1917" title="Western Front tactics, 1917">The British set-piece attack in late 1917</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
