<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Passchendaele,_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg/220px-The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg/330px-The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg/440px-The_Battle_of_Passchendaele%2C_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="641" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_of_Passchendaele,_July-november_1917_Q6416.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Derelict tank used as the roof of a dug out, Zillebeke, 20 September 1917 (Q6416)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/4th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="4th Army (German Empire)">4th Army</a> had held on to the Gheluvelt Plateau in August but its casualties worsened the German manpower shortage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007119.E2.80.93120_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007119.E2.80.93120-81">[78]</a></sup> Haig transferred the main offensive effort to the Second Army on 25 August and moved the northern boundary of the Second Army closer to the Ypres–Roulers railway. More heavy artillery was sent to Flanders from the armies further south and placed opposite the Gheluvelt Plateau.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962308_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962308-82">[79]</a></sup> Plumer continued the tactical development of the Fifth Army, during its slow and costly progress in August. After a pause of about three weeks, Plumer intended to capture the plateau in four steps, at six-day intervals to bring forward artillery and supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991237_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991237-83">[80]</a></sup> The Second Army attacks were to remain limited and infantry brigades re-organised to attack the first objective with a battalion each and the final one with two battalions, the opposite of the Fifth Army practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarble1998App_22_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarble1998App_22-84">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plumer arranged for the medium and heavy artillery reinforcements reaching Flanders to be added to the creeping bombardment, which had been impossible with the amount of artillery available to the Fifth Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarble1998App_22_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarble1998App_22-84">[81]</a></sup> The revised tactics were to ensure that more infantry attacked on narrower fronts, to a shallower depth than on of 31 July, like the Fifth Army attacks in August. The shorter and quicker advances were intended to be consolidated on tactically advantageous ground, especially reverse slopes, with the infantry still in contact with the artillery and aircraft, ready to repulse counter-attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962308_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962308-82">[79]</a></sup> The faster tempo of the operations was intended to add to German difficulties in replacing tired divisions through the railway bottlenecks behind the German front.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991236.E2.80.93242_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991236.E2.80.93242-85">[82]</a></sup> The pause in British attacks misled the Germans and Thaer at <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Gruppe Wijtschate</i></span>, wrote that it was <i>almost boring</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358-60">[57]</a></sup> Kuhl doubted that the offensive had ended but by 13 September changed his mind; two divisions, thirteen heavy batteries and twelve field batteries of artillery, three fighter squadrons and four other air force units were transferred from the 4th Army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977257_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977257-86">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="German_tactical_changes">German tactical changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: German tactical changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_tactics,_1917#German_defensive_changes:_late_1917" title="Western Front tactics, 1917">German defensive changes: late 1917</a></div>
<p>After setting objectives 1–2&#160;mi (1.6–3.2&#160;km) distant on 31 July, the British attempted shorter advances of approximately 1,500&#160;yd (1,400&#160;m) in August but were unable to achieve these lesser objectives on the south side of the battlefield because the rain soaked ground and poor visibility was to the advantage of the defenders. After the dry spell in early September, British advances had been much quicker and the final objective was reached a few hours after dawn, which confounded the German counter-attack divisions. Having crossed 2&#160;mi (3.2&#160;km) of mud, the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions found the British already dug in, with the German forward battle zone and its weak garrison gone beyond recapture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939303_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939303-87">[84]</a></sup> In August, German front-line divisions had two regiments deployed in the front line, with the third regiment in reserve. The front battalions had needed to be relieved much more frequently than expected, due to the power of British attacks, constant artillery-fire and the weather. Replacement units became mixed up with ones holding the front and reserve regiments had failed to intervene quickly, leaving front battalions unsupported until <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions arrived some hours later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers2010168_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers2010168-88">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July and August, German counter-attack (<span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span>) divisions had conducted an "advance to contact during mobile operations", which had given the Germans several costly defensive successes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007184_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007184-89">[86]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Menin_Road_Ridge" title="Battle of the Menin Road Ridge">Battle of the Menin Road Ridge</a>, German tactics were changed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers2010168_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers2010168-88">[85]</a></sup> After another defeat on 26 September, the German commanders made more tactical changes to counter the more conservative form of limited attacks adopted by the British.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007184_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007184-89">[86]</a></sup> German counter-attacks in September had been "assaults on reinforced field positions", due to the restrained nature of British infantry advances. The fine weather in early September had greatly eased British supply difficulties, especially in ammunition and the British made time to establish a defence in depth, protected by standing artillery barrages. The British attacks took place in dry, clear weather, with more aircraft over the battlefield for counter-attack reconnaissance, contact patrol and ground-attack operations. Systematic defensive artillery-fire was forfeited by the Germans, due to uncertainty over the position of their infantry, just when the British infantry benefited from the opposite. German counter-attacks were costly failures and on 28 September, Thaer wrote that the experience was "awful" and that he did not know what to do.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991294.E2.80.93295_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991294.E2.80.93295-90">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358-60">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ludendorff ordered the <i>Stellungsdivisionen</i> to reinforce their front garrisons; all machine-guns, including those of the support and reserve battalions were sent into the forward zone, to form a cordon of four to eight guns every 250&#160;yd (230&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939307.E2.80.93308_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939307.E2.80.93308-91">[88]</a></sup> The <i>Stellungsdivisionen</i> were reinforced by the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Stoss</i></span> regiments of <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions, which were moved into the artillery protective line behind the forward battle zone, to launch counter-attacks sooner. The other regiments of the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions were to be held back and used for a methodical counter-stroke a day or two after and spoiling attacks as the British reorganised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007190.E2.80.93191_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007190.E2.80.93191-92">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939307_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939307-93">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[d]</a></sup> More tactical changes were ordered on 30 September, operations to increase British infantry losses were to continue and gas bombardments were to be increased, weather permitting. Every effort was to be made to induce the British to reinforce their forward positions with infantry, where the German artillery could bombard them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007184.E2.80.93186_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007184.E2.80.93186-96">[92]</a></sup> Between 26 September and 3 October, the Germans attacked at least <span class="nowrap">24 times</span> and Operation High Storm <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Unternehmen Hohensturm</i></span>, a <i>Gegenangriff</i> (methodical counter-attack), to recapture the area around Zonnebeke was planned for 4 October.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977278_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977278-97">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson1996135_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson1996135-98">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_the_Menin_Road_Ridge">Battle of the Menin Road Ridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Battle of the Menin Road Ridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Menin_Road_Ridge" title="Battle of the Menin Road Ridge">Battle of the Menin Road Ridge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1264" data-file-height="964" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Menin_Road_-_wounded_at_side_of_the_road.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Wounded men at the side of a road after the Battle of Menin Road</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The British plan for the battle fought from <span class="nowrap">20–25 September,</span> included more emphasis on the use of heavy and medium artillery to destroy German concrete pill-boxes and machine-gun nests, which were more numerous in the battle zones being attacked and to engage in more counter-battery fire. The British had <span class="nowrap">575 heavy</span> and medium and <span class="nowrap">720 field</span> guns and howitzers, having more than doubled the quantity of artillery available at the Battle of Pilckem Ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991238.E2.80.93239_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991238.E2.80.93239-99">[95]</a></sup> Aircraft were to be used for systematic air observation of German troop movements, to avoid the failures of previous battles, where too few aircraft crews had been burdened with too many duties and had flown in bad weather.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1934181_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1934181-100">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 20 September, the Allies attacked on a 14,500&#160;yd (13,300&#160;m) front and captured most of their objectives, to a depth of about 1,500&#160;yd (1,400&#160;m) by mid-morning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977261_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977261-101">[97]</a></sup> The Germans made many counter-attacks, beginning around <span class="nowrap">3:00 p.m.</span> until early evening, all of which failed to gain ground or made only a temporary penetration of the new British positions. The German defence had failed to stop a well-prepared attack made in good weather.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris2008366_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris2008366-102">[98]</a></sup> Minor attacks took place after 20 September, as both sides jockeyed for position and reorganised their defences. A mutually-costly attack by the Germans on 25 September, recaptured pillboxes at the south western end of Polygon Wood. Next day, the German positions near the wood were swept away in the Battle of Polygon Wood.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007165_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007165-103">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="German_counter-attack,_25_September"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="German_counter-attack.2C_25_September">German counter-attack, 25 September</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: German counter-attack, 25 September">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Action_of_25_September_1917" title="Action of 25 September 1917">Action of 25 September 1917</a></div>
<p>Two regiments of the German <a href="/wiki/50th_Reserve_Division_(German_Empire)" title="50th Reserve Division (German Empire)">50th Reserve Division</a> attacked on a 1,800&#160;yd (1,600&#160;m) front, either side of the Reutelbeek, supported by aircraft and <span class="nowrap">44 field</span> and <span class="nowrap">20 heavy</span> batteries of artillery, four times the usual amount for a division. The German infantry managed to advance on the flanks, for about 100&#160;yd (91&#160;m) near the Menin road and 600&#160;yd (550&#160;m) north of the Reutelbeek. The infantry were supported by artillery-observation and ground-attack aircraft and a box-barrage fired behind the British front-line, which isolated the British from reinforcements and ammunition. Return-fire from the <a href="/wiki/33rd_Division_(United_Kingdom)" title="33rd Division (United Kingdom)">33rd Division</a> and the <a href="/wiki/15th_Brigade_(Australia)" title="15th Brigade (Australia)">15th Australian Brigade</a> of the <a href="/wiki/5th_Division_(Australia)" title="5th Division (Australia)">5th Australian Division</a> along the southern edge of Polygon wood, forced the attackers under cover around some of the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Wilhelm Stellung</i></span> pillboxes, near Black Watch Corner, at the south-western edge of Polygon Wood. German attempts to reinforce the attacking troops failed, due to British artillery observers isolating the advanced German troops with artillery barrages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991282.E2.80.93284_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991282.E2.80.93284-104">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plumer ordered the attack on 26 September to go ahead but reduced the objectives of the 33rd Division. The 98th Brigade was to advance and cover the right flank of the 5th Australian Division and the 100th Brigade was to re-capture the lost ground further south. The 5th Australian Division advance the next day began with uncertainty as to the security of the right flank; the attack of the depleted 98th Brigade was delayed and only managed to reach Black Watch Corner, 1,000&#160;yd (910&#160;m) short of its objectives. Reinforcements moved into the 5th Australian Division area and attacked south-westwards at noon, as a frontal attack was made from Black Watch Corner without artillery support, because British troops were known to be holding out. The attack succeeded by <span class="nowrap">2:00 p.m.;</span> later in the afternoon, the 100th Brigade re-took the ground lost north of the Menin road. Casualties in the 33rd Division were so great that it was relieved on 27 September by the 23rd Division, which had only been withdrawn on the night of <span class="nowrap">24/25 September.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991286.E2.80.93287_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991286.E2.80.93287-105">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Polygon_Wood">Battle of Polygon Wood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Battle of Polygon Wood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Polygon_Wood" title="Battle of Polygon Wood">Battle of Polygon Wood</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg/170px-Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg/255px-Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg/340px-Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1607" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Australian_infantry_small_box_respirators_Ypres_1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Australian infantry with small box respirator gas masks, Ypres, September 1917</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Second Army altered its Corps frontages soon after the attack of 20 September, for the next effort <span class="nowrap">(26 September – 3 October)</span> so that each attacking division could be concentrated on a 1,000&#160;yd (910&#160;m) front. Roads and light railways were extended to the new front line, to allow artillery and ammunition to be moved forward. The artillery of <a href="/wiki/VIII_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="VIII Corps (United Kingdom)">VIII Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/IX_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="IX Corps (United Kingdom)">IX Corps</a> on the southern flank, simulated preparations for attacks on Zandvoorde and Warneton. At <span class="nowrap">5.50 a.m.</span> on 26 September, five layers of barrage fired by British artillery and machine-guns began. Dust and smoke thickened the morning mist and the infantry advanced using compass bearings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991284_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991284-106">[102]</a></sup> Each of the three German ground-holding divisions attacked on 26 September, had an <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> division in support, twice the ratio of 20 September. No ground captured by the British was lost and German counter-attacks managed only to reach ground to which survivors of the front-line divisions had retired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991293_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991293-107">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="October–November"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="October.E2.80.93November">October–November</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: October–November">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="German_counter-attacks,_30_September_–_4_October"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="German_counter-attacks.2C_30_September_.E2.80.93_4_October">German counter-attacks, 30 September – 4 October</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: German counter-attacks, 30 September – 4 October">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Actions_of_30_September_%E2%80%93_4_October_1917" title="Actions of 30 September – 4 October 1917">Actions of 30 September – 4 October 1917</a></div>
<p>At <span class="nowrap">4:00 a.m.</span> on 30 September, a thick mist covered the ground and at <span class="nowrap">4:30 a.m.</span> German artillery began a bombardment between the Menin road and the Reutelbeek. At <span class="nowrap">5:15 a.m.,</span> German troops emerged from the mist on an 800&#160;yd (730&#160;m) front.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandilands2003198.E2.80.93199_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandilands2003198.E2.80.93199-108">[104]</a></sup> The attack was supported by flame-throwers and German infantry throwing smoke- and hand-grenades. The British replied with small-arms fire and bombs, forcing the Germans to retreat in confusion but a post was lost south of the Menin road, then retaken by an immediate counter-attack. SOS rockets were not seen in the mist and the British artillery remained silent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991301.2C_302_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991301.2C_302-109">[105]</a></sup> The Germans were repulsed again at <span class="nowrap">6:00 a.m.</span> but German artillery-fire continued during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandilands2003198.E2.80.93199_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandilands2003198.E2.80.93199-108">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 October, at <span class="nowrap">5:00 a.m.,</span> a German hurricane bombardment began from the Reutelbeek north to Polygon Wood and Black Watch Corner; by coincidence a Second Army practice barrage began at <span class="nowrap">5:15 a.m.</span> The British front line was cut off and German infantry attacked in three waves at <span class="nowrap">5:30 a.m.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941837_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941837-110">[106]</a></sup> Two determined German attacks were repulsed south of Cameron Covert, then at <span class="nowrap">7:00 p.m.</span> German troops massed near the Menin road. The German attack was defeated by small-arms fire and the British artillery, whose observers had seen the SOS rockets. The British were forced out of Cameron Covert and counter-attacked but a German attack began at the same time and the British were repulsed. Another German attack failed and the German troops dug in behind some old German barbed wire; after dark, more German attacks around Cameron Covert failed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandilands2003200.E2.80.93204_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandilands2003200.E2.80.93204-111">[107]</a></sup> North of the covert near Polygon Wood, deep mud smothered German shells before they exploded but they still caused many casualties. Communication with the rear was lost and the Germans attacked all day but British SOS rockets remained visible and the attacks took no ground; after dark German attacks were repulsed by another three SOS barrages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAtkinson2009410.E2.80.93412_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAtkinson2009410.E2.80.93412-112">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p><span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Unternehmen Hohensturm</i></span> (Operation High Storm) was planned by <i>Gruppe Ypres</i> to recapture the Tokio Spur from Zonnebeke south to Molenaarelsthoek at the eastern edge of Polygon Wood on 3 October.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941846_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941846-113">[109]</a></sup> The attacking infantry from the 45th Reserve and the 4th Guard divisions were commanded by Major Freiherr von Schleinitz in the north and Lieutenant-Colonel Rave in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941847_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941847-114">[110]</a></sup> After the costly failure of the <i>Gegenangriff</i> on 1 October, the attack was put back to 4 October, rehearsals taking place from 2 to 3 October.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991303.E2.80.93304_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991303.E2.80.93304-115">[111]</a></sup> On the night of <span class="nowrap">3/4 October,</span> the German commanders had doubts about the attack but decided to proceed with the <i>Gegenangriff</i>, warning the artillery to be ready to commence defensive bombardments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941858_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941858-116">[112]</a></sup> A contact patrol aircraft was arranged to fly over the area at <span class="nowrap">7:30 a.m.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941847_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941847-114">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Broodseinde">Battle of Broodseinde</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Battle of Broodseinde">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Broodseinde" title="Battle of Broodseinde">Battle of Broodseinde</a></div>
<p>On 4 October, the British began the Battle of Broodseinde to complete the capture of the Gheluvelt Plateau and occupy Broodseinde Ridge. By coincidence, the Germans sought to recapture their defences around Zonnebeke with a methodical counter-attack (<i>Gegenangriff</i>) at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson1996135_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson1996135-98">[94]</a></sup> The British attacked along a 14,000&#160;yd (13,000&#160;m) front and as the I Anzac Corps divisions began their advance towards Broodseinde Ridge, men were seen rising from shell-holes in no man's land and more German troops were found concealed in shell-craters. Most of the German troops of the <a href="/wiki/45th_Reserve_Division_(German_Empire)" title="45th Reserve Division (German Empire)">45th Reserve Division</a> were overrun or retreated through the British barrage, then the Australians attacked pillboxes one-by-one and captured the village of Zonnebeke north of the ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941837.2C_847_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941837.2C_847-117">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991304.E2.80.93307_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991304.E2.80.93307-118">[114]</a></sup> When the British barrage began on Broodseinde Ridge, the Keiberg Spur and Waterdamhoek, some of the German forward headquarters staffs only realised that they were under attack when British and Australian troops appeared.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941858.E2.80.93859_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941858.E2.80.93859-119">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>As news arrived of the great success of the attack, the head of GHQ Intelligence went to the Second Army headquarters to discuss exploitation. Plumer declined the suggestion, as eight fresh German divisions were behind the battlefield, with another six beyond them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991316_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991316-120">[116]</a></sup> Later in the day, Plumer had second thoughts and ordered I Anzac Corps to push on to the Keiberg spur, with support from the II Anzac Corps. The II Anzac Corps commander wanted to advance north-east towards Passchendaele village but the I Anzac Corps commander preferred to wait until artillery had been brought up and supply routes improved. The X Corps commander proposed an attack northward from In de Ster into the southern flank of the Germans opposite I Anzac Corps. The 7th Division commander objected, due to uncertainty about the situation and the many casualties suffered by the 21st Division on the right flank and Plumer changed his mind again. During the morning, Gough had told the Fifth Army corps commanders to push on but when reports arrived of a repulse at 19 Metre Hill, the order was cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991315.E2.80.93317_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991315.E2.80.93317-121">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="German_defensive_changes">German defensive changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: German defensive changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_tactics,_1917#German_defensive_changes:_late_1917" title="Western Front tactics, 1917">German defensive changes: late 1917</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg/220px-NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg/330px-NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg/440px-NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9440" data-file-height="7295" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NLS_Haig_-_Troops_moving_up_at_eventide_-_men_of_a_Yorkshire_regiment_on_the_march.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">British soldiers moving forward during the Battle of Broodseinde. Photo by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Brooks_(photographer)" title="Ernest Brooks (photographer)">Ernest Brooks</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 7 October, the 4th Army again dispersed its troops in the front defence zone. Reserve battalions moved back behind the artillery protective line and the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions were organised to intervene as swiftly as possible once an attack commenced, despite the risk of British artillery-fire. Counter-battery fire to suppress the British artillery was to be increased, to protect the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Eingreif</i></span> divisions as they advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939309_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939309-122">[118]</a></sup> All of the German divisions holding front zones were relieved and an extra division brought forward, because the British advances had lengthened the front line. Without the divisions necessary for a counter-offensive south of the Gheluvelt Plateau towards Kemmel Hill, Rupprecht began to plan for a slow withdrawal from the Ypres Salient, even at the risk of uncovering German positions further north and on the Belgian coast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007228.E2.80.93229_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007228.E2.80.93229-123">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[e]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Poelcappelle">Battle of Poelcappelle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Battle of Poelcappelle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Poelcappelle" title="Battle of Poelcappelle">Battle of Poelcappelle</a></div>
<p>The French First Army and British Second and Fifth armies attacked on 9 October, on a 13,500&#160;yd (12,300&#160;m) front, from south of Broodseinde to St Jansbeek, to advance half of the distance from Broodseinde ridge to Passchendaele, on the main front, which led to many casualties on both sides. Advances in the north of the attack front were retained by British and French troops but most of the ground taken in front of Passchendaele and on the Becelaere and Gheluvelt spurs was lost to German counter-attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941887_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941887-125">[120]</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/William_Birdwood,_1st_Baron_Birdwood" class="mw-redirect" title="William Birdwood, 1st Baron Birdwood">William Birdwood</a> later wrote that the return of heavy rain and mud sloughs was the main cause of the failure to hold captured ground. Kuhl concluded that the fighting strained German fighting power to the limit but that the German forces managed to prevent a breakthrough, although it was becoming much harder to replace losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977287.E2.80.93288_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977287.E2.80.93288-126">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Battle_of_Passchendaele">First Battle of Passchendaele</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: First Battle of Passchendaele">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="First Battle of Passchendaele">First Battle of Passchendaele</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg/220px-Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg/330px-Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg/440px-Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="674" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passchendaele_aerial_view.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Aerial view of Passchendaele village before and after the battle</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The First Battle of Passchendaele on 12 October was another Allied attempt to gain ground around Passchendaele. Heavy rain and mud again made movement difficult and little artillery could be brought closer to the front. Allied troops were exhausted and morale had fallen. After a modest British advance, German counter-attacks recovered most of the ground lost opposite Passchendaele, except for an area on the right of the Wallemolen spur. North of Poelcappelle, the XIV Corps of the Fifth Army advanced along the Broembeek some way up the Watervlietbeek and the Stadenrevebeek streams and the Guards Division captured the west end of the Vijwegen spur, gaining observation over the south end of Houthulst Forest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991341.E2.80.93344_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991341.E2.80.93344-127">[122]</a></sup> There were <span class="nowrap">13,000 Allied</span> casualties, including <span class="nowrap">2,735 New</span> Zealanders, <span class="nowrap">845 of</span> whom were dead or stranded in the mud of no-man's-land; it was one of the worst days in New Zealand military history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle1997285_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle1997285-128">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>At a conference on 13 October, Haig and the army commanders agreed that attacks would stop until the weather improved and roads could be extended, to carry more artillery and ammunition forward. The offensive was to continue, to reach a suitable line for the winter and to keep German attention on Flanders, with a French attack due on 23 October and the Third Army operation south of Arras scheduled for mid-November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991345.E2.80.93346_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991345.E2.80.93346-129">[124]</a></sup> The battle was also costly for the Germans, who lost more than <span class="nowrap">1,000 prisoners.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoraston1919130_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoraston1919130-130">[125]</a></sup> The German 195th Division at Passchendaele suffered <span class="nowrap">3,325</span> casualties from 9 to 12 October and had to be relieved by the 238th Division.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007236_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007236-131">[126]</a></sup> Ludendorff became optimistic that Passchendaele Ridge could be held and ordered the 4th Army to stand fast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007233_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007233-132">[127]</a></sup> On 18 October, Kuhl advocated a retreat as far to the east as possible; Armin and Lossberg wanted to hold on, because the ground beyond the Passchendaele <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">watershed</a> was untenable, even in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977305_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977305-133">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Action_of_22_October">Action of 22 October</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Action of 22 October">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Action_of_22_October_1917" title="Action of 22 October 1917">Action of 22 October 1917</a></div>
<p>On 22 October the 18th (Eastern) Division of XVIII Corps attacked the east end of Poelcappelle as XIV Corps to the north attacked with the 34th Division between the Watervlietbeek and Broenbeek streams and the 35th Division northwards into <a href="/wiki/Houthulst" title="Houthulst">Houthulst Forest</a>. The attack was supported by a regiment of the French 1st Division on the left flank of the 35th Division and was intended to obstruct a possible German counter-attack on the left flank of the Canadian Corps as it attacked Passchendaele and the ridge. The artillery of the Second and Fifth armies conducted a bombardment to simulate a general attack as a deception. Poelcappelle was captured but the attack at the junction between the 34th and 35th divisions was repulsed. German counter-attacks pushed back the 35th Division in the centre but the French attack captured all its objectives. Attacking on ground cut up by bombardments and soaked by rain, the British had struggled to advance in places and lost the ability to move quickly to outflank pillboxes. The 35th Division reached the fringe of Houthulst Forest but was outflanked and pushed back in places. German counter-attacks made after 22 October, were at an equal disadvantage and were costly failures. The German 4th Army was prevented from transferring troops away from the Fifth Army and from concentrating its artillery-fire on the Canadians as they prepared for the Second Battle of Passchendaele (26 October – 10 November 1917).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerry2014475.E2.80.93486_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerry2014475.E2.80.93486-134">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_La_Malmaison">Battle of La Malmaison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Battle of La Malmaison">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Malmaison" title="Battle of La Malmaison">Battle of La Malmaison</a></div>
<p>After numerous requests from Haig, Petain began the Battle of La Malmaison, a long-delayed French attack on the Chemin des Dames, by the Sixth Army (General <a href="/wiki/Paul_Maistre" title="Paul Maistre">Paul Maistre</a>). The artillery preparation started on 17 October and on 23 October, the German defenders were swiftly defeated and the French advanced up to 3.7&#160;mi (6.0&#160;km), capturing the village and fort of La Malmaison, gaining control of the Chemin des Dames ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977307_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977307-135">[130]</a></sup> The Germans lost <span class="nowrap">38,000 men</span> killed or missing and <span class="nowrap">12,000 prisoners,</span> along with <span class="nowrap">200 guns</span> and <span class="nowrap">720 machine-guns,</span> against <span class="nowrap">14,000 French</span> casualties, fewer than a third of the German total.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhilpott2014279_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhilpott2014279-136">[131]</a></sup> The Germans had to withdraw from their remaining positions on the Chemin des Dames to the north of the Ailette Valley early in November. Haig was pleased with the French success but regretted the delay, which had lessened its effect on the Flanders operations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977307_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977307-135">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele">Second Battle of Passchendaele</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Second Battle of Passchendaele">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="Second Battle of Passchendaele">Second Battle of Passchendaele</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg/220px-Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg/330px-Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg/440px-Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2637" data-file-height="2155" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele_-_Barbed_wire_and_Mud.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Terrain through which the Canadian Corps advanced at Passchendaele, in late 1917</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg/220px-Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg/330px-Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg/440px-Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1556" data-file-height="1167" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landscape_Passendale_Tyne_Cot_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Terrain at Passchendaele near where the Canadian Corps advanced, spring 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The British <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Fifth Army (United Kingdom)">Fifth Army</a> undertook minor operations from <span class="nowrap">20–22 October,</span> to maintain pressure on the Germans and support the French attack at La Malmaison, while the Canadian Corps prepared for a series of attacks from <span class="nowrap">26 October – 10 November.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941930_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941930-137">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991347_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991347-138">[133]</a></sup> The four divisions of the Canadian Corps had been transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Ypres_Salient" title="Ypres Salient">Ypres Salient</a> from Lens, to capture Passchendaele and the ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941929_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941929-139">[134]</a></sup> The Canadians relieved the <a href="/wiki/II_Anzac_Corps" class="mw-redirect" title="II Anzac Corps">II Anzac Corps</a> on 18 October and found that the front line was mostly the same as that occupied by the <a href="/wiki/1st_Canadian_Division" title="1st Canadian Division">1st Canadian Division</a> in April 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962312_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962312-140">[135]</a></sup> The Canadian operation was to be three limited attacks, on 26 October, 30 October and 6 November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962314_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962314-141">[136]</a></sup> On 26 October, the 3rd Canadian Division captured its objective at Wolf Copse, then swung back its northern flank to link with the adjacent division of the Fifth Army. The 4th Canadian Division captured its objectives but was forced slowly to retire from Decline Copse, against German counter-attacks and communication failures between the Canadian and Australian units to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962320_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962320-142">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second stage began on 30 October, to complete the previous stage and gain a base for the final assault on Passchendaele.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962320_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962320-142">[137]</a></sup> The attackers on the southern flank quickly captured Crest Farm and sent patrols beyond the final objective into Passchendaele. The attack on the northern flank again met with exceptional German resistance. The 3rd Canadian Division captured Vapour Farm on the corps boundary, Furst Farm to the west of Meetcheele and the crossroads at Meetcheele but remained short of its objective.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962321_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962321-143">[138]</a></sup> During a seven-day pause, the Second Army took over another section of the Fifth Army front adjoining the Canadian Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962323_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962323-144">[139]</a></sup> Three rainless days from <span class="nowrap">3–5 November</span> eased preparation for the next stage, which began on the morning of 6 November, with the 1st Canadian Division and the <a href="/wiki/2nd_Canadian_Division" title="2nd Canadian Division">2nd Canadian Division</a>. In fewer than three hours, many units reached their final objectives and Passchendaele was captured. The Canadian Corps launched a final action on 10 November, to gain control of the remaining high ground north of the village near <span class="nowrap">Hill 52.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1962325_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1962325-145">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007311.E2.80.93312_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007311.E2.80.93312-146">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[f]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="December">December</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: December">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Night_action_of_1/2_December_1917"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Night_action_of_1.2F2_December_1917">Night action of 1/2 December 1917</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Night action of 1/2 December 1917">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Night_action_of_1/2_December_1917" title="Night action of 1/2 December 1917">Night action of 1/2 December 1917</a></div>
<p>On 18 November the VIII Corps on the right and II Corps on the left (northern) side of the Passchendaele Salient took over from the Canadian Corps. The area was subjected to constant German artillery bombardments and its vulnerability to attack led to a suggestion by Brigadier C. F. Aspinall, that either the British should retire to the west side of the Gheluvelt Plateau or advance to broaden the salient towards Westroosebeke. Expanding the salient would make the troops in it less vulnerable to German artillery-fire and provide a better jumping off line for a resumption of the offensive in the spring of 1918. The British attacked towards Westroozebeke on the night of 1/2 December but the plan to mislead the Germans by not bombarding the German defences until eight minutes after the infantry began their advance came undone. The noise of the British assembly and the difficulty of moving across muddy and waterlogged ground had also alerted the Germans. In the moonlight, the Germans had seen the British troops when they were still 200&#160;yd (180&#160;m) away. Some ground was captured and about <span class="nowrap">150 prisoners</span> were taken but the attack on the redoubts failed and observation over the heads of the valleys on the east and north sides of the ridge was not achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoCicero2011155.E2.80.93338_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoCicero2011155.E2.80.93338-148">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Action_on_the_Polderhoek_Spur">Action on the Polderhoek Spur</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Action on the Polderhoek Spur">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Action_on_the_Polderhoek_Spur" title="Action on the Polderhoek Spur">Action on the Polderhoek Spur</a></div>
<p>The attack on the Polderhoek Spur on 3 December 1917, was a local operation by the British <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Army_(United_Kingdom)" title="Fourth Army (United Kingdom)">Fourth Army</a> (renamed from the Second Army on 8 November). Two battalions of the <a href="/wiki/2nd_Infantry_Brigade_(New_Zealand)" title="2nd Infantry Brigade (New Zealand)">2nd New Zealand Brigade</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Division" title="New Zealand Division">New Zealand Division</a> attacked the low ridge, from which German observers could view the area from Cameron Covert to the north and the Menin road to the south-west. A New Zealand advance of 600&#160;yd (550&#160;m) on a 400&#160;yd (370&#160;m) front, would shield the area north of the Reutelbeek stream from German observers on the Gheluvelt spur. Heavy artillery bombarded the ruins of Polderhoek Château and the pillboxes in the grounds to mislead the defenders and the attack was made in daylight as a ruse to surprise the Germans, who would be under cover sheltering from the routine bombardments. Smoke and gas bombardments on the Gheluvelt and Becelaere spurs on the flanks and the infantry attack began at the same time as the "routine" bombardment. The ruse failed, some British artillery-fire dropped short on the New Zealanders and the Germans engaged the attackers with small-arms fire from Polderhoek Spur and Gheluvelt ridge. A strong west wind ruined the smoke screens and the British artillery failed to suppress the German machine-guns. New Zealand machine-gunners repulsed a counter-attack but the New Zealand infantry were 150&#160;yd (140&#160;m) short of the first objective; another attempt after dark was cancelled because of the full moon and the arrival of German reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart2014304.E2.80.93314_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart2014304.E2.80.93314-149">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>In a German General Staff publication, it was written that "Germany had been brought near to certain destruction (<span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>sicheren Untergang</i></span>) by the Flanders battle of 1917".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991xiii_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991xiii-152">[145]</a></sup> In his Memoirs of 1938, Lloyd George wrote, "Passchendaele was indeed one of the greatest disasters of the war&#160;... No soldier of any intelligence now defends this senseless campaign&#160;...".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977xix.E2.80.93xx_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977xix.E2.80.93xx-153">[146]</a></sup> In 1939, G. C. Wynne wrote that the British had eventually reached Passchendaele Ridge and captured <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern I Stellung</i></span>; beyond them were <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern II</i></span> and <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Flandern III</i></span> (which was nearing completion). The German submarine bases on the coast had not been captured but the objective of diverting the Germans from the French further south, while they recovered from the Nivelle Offensive in April, had succeeded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1939214.E2.80.93215_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1939214.E2.80.93215-154">[147]</a></sup> In 1997, Griffith wrote that the <i>bite and hold</i> system kept moving until November, because the BEF had developed a workable system of offensive tactics, against which the Germans ultimately had no answer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle199771_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle199771-155">[148]</a></sup> A decade later, Sheldon wrote that relative casualty figures were irrelevant, because the German army could not afford great numbers of losses or to lose the initiative by being compelled to fight another defensive battle, on ground of the Allies' choosing. The Third Battle of Ypres pinned the German army to Flanders and caused unsustainable casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007313.E2.80.93317_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007313.E2.80.93317-156">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>At a conference on 13 October, a scheme of the Third Army for an attack in mid-November was discussed. General <a href="/wiki/Julian_Byng,_1st_Viscount_Byng_of_Vimy" title="Julian Byng, 1st Viscount Byng of Vimy">Julian Byng</a>, commander of the Third Army, wanted the operations at Ypres to continue, to hold German troops in Flanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991345.E2.80.93346_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991345.E2.80.93346-129">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cambrai_(1917)" title="Battle of Cambrai (1917)">Battle of Cambrai</a> began on 20 November, when the British breached the first two parts of the <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_Line" title="Hindenburg Line">Hindenburg Line</a>, in the first successful mass use of <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">tanks</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Combined_arms" title="Combined arms">combined arms</a> operation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1995124.E2.80.93125_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1995124.E2.80.93125-157">[150]</a></sup> The experience of the failure to contain the British attacks at Ypres and the drastic reduction in areas of the western front that could be considered "quiet" after the tank and artillery surprise at Cambrai, left the OHL with little choice but to return to a strategy of decisive victory in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2009312_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2009312-158">[151]</a></sup> On 24 October, the Austro-German 14th Army (<span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>General der Infanterie</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Below" title="Otto von Below">Otto von Below</a>), attacked the Italian Second Army on the Isonzo, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Caporetto" title="Battle of Caporetto">Battle of Caporetto</a> and in 18 days, inflicted casualties of <span class="nowrap">650,000 men</span> and <span class="nowrap">3,000 guns.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles194815_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles194815-159">[152]</a></sup> In fear that Italy might be put out of the war, the French and British Governments offered reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941935.E2.80.93936_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941935.E2.80.93936-160">[153]</a></sup> British and French troops were swiftly moved from <span class="nowrap">10 November – 12 December</span> but the diversion of resources from the BEF forced Haig to conclude the Third Battle of Ypres short of <a href="/wiki/Westrozebeke" title="Westrozebeke">Westrozebeke</a>, the last substantial British attack being made on 10 November.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBean1941936_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBean1941936-161">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties">Casualties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Casualties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various casualty figures have been published, sometimes with acrimony but the highest estimates for British and German casualties appear to be discredited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcRandleQuirk2006667.E2.80.93701_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcRandleQuirk2006667.E2.80.93701-162">[155]</a></sup> In the Official History, Brigadier-General <a href="/wiki/James_E._Edmonds" class="mw-redirect" title="James E. Edmonds">J. E. Edmonds</a> put British casualties <span class="nowrap">at 244,897</span> and wrote that equivalent German figures were not available, estimating German losses <span class="nowrap">at 400,000.</span> Edmonds considered that <span class="nowrap">30 percent needed</span> to be added to German figures, to make them comparable to British casualty criteria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991361.E2.80.93363_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991361.E2.80.93363-163">[156]</a></sup> In 2007, Sheldon wrote that although German casualties from <span class="nowrap">1 June – 10 November</span> <span class="nowrap">were 217,194,</span> a figure available in Volume III of the <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Sanitätsbericht</i></span> (1934), Edmonds may not have included them as they did not fit his case. Sheldon recorded <span class="nowrap">182,396 slightly</span> wounded and sick soldiers <i>not struck off unit strength</i>, which if included would make <span class="nowrap">399,590 German</span> losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007313.E2.80.93315_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007313.E2.80.93315-164">[157]</a></sup> The British claim to have taken <span class="nowrap">24,065 prisoners</span> has not been disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoraston1919133_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoraston1919133-165">[158]</a></sup> In 1940, <a href="/wiki/C._R._M._F._Cruttwell" title="C. R. M. F. Cruttwell">C. R. M. F. Cruttwell</a> recorded <span class="nowrap">300,000 British</span> casualties and <span class="nowrap">400,000 German.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECruttwell1940442_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECruttwell1940442-166">[159]</a></sup> Wolff in 1958 gave German casualties as <span class="nowrap">270,713</span> and <span class="nowrap">448,688 British.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolff1958259_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolff1958259-167">[160]</a></sup> In 1959, <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Falls" title="Cyril Falls">Cyril Falls</a> estimated <span class="nowrap">240,000 British,</span> <span class="nowrap">8,525 French</span> and <span class="nowrap">260,000 German</span> casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalls1959303_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalls1959303-168">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Terraine" title="John Terraine">John Terraine</a> followed Falls in 1963 but did not accept that German losses were as high <span class="nowrap">as 400,000.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1963372_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1963372-169">[162]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> in 1972, wrote that the Official History had performed a "conjuring trick" on these figures and that no one believed these "farcical calculations". Taylor put British wounded and killed at <span class="nowrap">300,000 and</span> German losses at <span class="nowrap">200,000.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor1972181.E2.80.93182_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor1972181.E2.80.93182-170">[163]</a></sup> In 1977, Terraine argued that twenty percent needed to be added to the German figures for some lightly wounded men, who would have been included under British definitions of casualties, making German casualties <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 260,400.</span> Terraine refuted Wolff (1958), who despite writing that <span class="nowrap">448,614 British</span> casualties was the BEF total for the second half of 1917, neglected to deduct <span class="nowrap">75,681 casualties</span> for the Battle of Cambrai, given in the <i>Official Statistics</i> from which he quoted or "normal wastage", averaging <span class="nowrap">35,000 per</span> month in "quiet" periods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine1977344.E2.80.93345_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine1977344.E2.80.93345-171">[164]</a></sup> Prior and Wilson in 1997, gave British losses as <span class="nowrap">275,000 and</span> German casualties at just <span class="nowrap">under 200,000.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson1996195_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson1996195-172">[165]</a></sup> Hagenlücke in 1997, gave <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 217,000 German</span> casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle199745.E2.80.9358-60">[57]</a></sup> Sheffield wrote in 2002, that <a href="/wiki/Richard_Holmes_(military_historian)" title="Richard Holmes (military historian)">Richard Holmes's</a> guess of <span class="nowrap">260,000 casualties</span> on each side, seemed about right.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield2001216_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield2001216-173">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_operations">Subsequent operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Subsequent operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Winter,_1917–1918"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Winter.2C_1917.E2.80.931918">Winter, 1917–1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Winter, 1917–1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The area to the east and south of Passchendaele was held by posts, those to the east being fairly habitable, unlike the southern ones; from Passchendaele as far back as Potijze, the ground was far worse. Each brigade spent four days in the front line, four in support and four in reserve. The area was quiet apart from artillery-fire and in December the weather turned cold and snowy, which entailed a great effort to prevent <a href="/wiki/Trench_foot" title="Trench foot">trench foot</a>. In January, spells of freezing cold were followed by warmer periods, one beginning on 15 January with torrential rain and gale-force winds, washing away plank roads and <a href="/wiki/Duckboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Duckboard">duckboard</a> tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorastonBax1999167.E2.80.93168_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorastonBax1999167.E2.80.93168-174">[167]</a></sup> Conditions in the salient improved with the completion of transport routes and the refurbishment of German pillboxes. Both sides raided and the British used night machine-gun fire and artillery barrages to great effect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeton_Hutchinson200579.E2.80.9380_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeton_Hutchinson200579.E2.80.9380-175">[168]</a></sup> On the evening of 3 March 1918, two companies of the 8th Division raided Teal Cottage, supported by a smoke and shrapnel barrage, killed many of the garrison and took six prisoners for one man wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorastonBax1999171_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorastonBax1999171-176">[169]</a></sup> A German attack on 11 March was repulsed; after that the Germans made no more attacks, keeping up frequent artillery bombardments and machine-gun fire instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillon2002180.E2.80.93183_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillon2002180.E2.80.93183-177">[170]</a></sup> When the German armies further south began the <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensive</a> on 21 March 1918, "good" divisions in Flanders were sent south; the 29th Division was withdrawn on 9 April and transferred to the Lys.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeton_Hutchinson200580_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeton_Hutchinson200580-178">[171]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGillon2002186_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGillon2002186-179">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Retreat,_1918"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Retreat.2C_1918">Retreat, 1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Retreat, 1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Michael" title="Operation Michael">Operation Michael</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Lys_(1918)" title="Battle of the Lys (1918)">Battle of the Lys (1918)</a></div>
<p>On 23 March, Haig ordered Plumer to make contingency plans to shorten the line and release troops for the other armies. Worn-out divisions from the south had been sent to Flanders to recuperate closer to the coast. On 11 April, Plumer authorised a withdrawal of the southern flank of the Second Army. On 12 April, the VIII Corps HQ ordered the infantry retirement to begin that night and the 59th Division was replaced by part of the 41st Division and transferred south. The II Corps had begun to withdraw its artillery at the same time as VIII Corps on the night of 11/12 April and ordered the 36th and 30th divisions to conform to the VIII Corps retirement, which were completed by 13 April, without German interference.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmondsDaviesMaxwell-Hyslop1995113.E2.80.93114.2C_245.2C_275_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmondsDaviesMaxwell-Hyslop1995113.E2.80.93114.2C_245.2C_275-180">[173]</a></sup> On 13 April, Plumer agreed to a retirement in the south side of the salient to a line from Mt Kemmel to Voormezeele (2.5&#160;mi (4.0&#160;km) south of Ypres), White Château (1&#160;mi (1.6&#160;km) east of Ypres) and Pilckem Ridge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmondsDaviesMaxwell-Hyslop1995299.E2.80.93300.2C_319.2C_316.2C_326_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmondsDaviesMaxwell-Hyslop1995299.E2.80.93300.2C_319.2C_316.2C_326-181">[174]</a></sup> The 4th Army diary recorded that the withdrawal was discovered at <span class="nowrap">4;40 a.m.</span> Next day, at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Merckem&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Battle of Merckem (page does not exist)">Battle of Merckem</a>, the Germans attacked north-east of Ypres, from Houthulst Forest and captured Kippe but were forced out by Belgian counter-attacks, supported by the II Corps artillery. On the afternoon of 27 April, the south end of the Second Army outpost line was driven in near Voormezeele and another British outpost line was established north-east of the village.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmondsDaviesMaxwell-Hyslop1995337.E2.80.93338.2C_342.2C_443_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmondsDaviesMaxwell-Hyslop1995337.E2.80.93338.2C_342.2C_443-182">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Passchendaele&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG/220px-View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG/330px-View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG/440px-View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_top_of_Tyne_Cot.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Tyne Cot Commonwealth War Graves Cemetery and Memorial to the Missing</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Menin_Gate" title="Menin Gate">Menin Gate Memorial to the Missing</a> commemorates those of all Commonwealth nations (except New Zealand) who died in the Ypres Salient and have no known grave. In the case of the United Kingdom only casualties before 16 August 1917 are commemorated on the memorial. United Kingdom and New Zealand servicemen who died after that date are named on the memorial at <a href="/wiki/Tyne_Cot_Cemetery" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyne Cot Cemetery">Tyne Cot Cemetery</a>. There is a New Zealand Memorial marking where New Zealand troops fought at Gravenstafel Ridge on 4 October, located on Roeselarestraat.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[176]</a></sup> There are numerous tributes and memorials in Australia and New Zealand to Anzac soldiers who died in the battle, including plaques at the <a href="/wiki/Christchurch" title="Christchurch">Christchurch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dunedin" title="Dunedin">Dunedin</a> railway stations. The Canadian Corps' participation in the Second Battle of Passchendaele is commemorated with the <a href="/wiki/Passchendaele_Memorial" title="Passchendaele Memorial">Passchendaele Memorial</a> at site of the Crest Farm on the south-west fringe of Passchendaele village.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVance199766_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVance199766-184">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the newest monuments to be dedicated to the fighting contribution of a group is the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic Cross">Celtic Cross</a> memorial, commemorating the Scottish contribution to the fighting in Flanders during the Great War. This memorial is on Frezenberg Ridge where the 9th (Scottish) Division and the 15th (Scottish) Division fought during the Third Battle of Ypres. The monument was dedicated by <a href="/wiki/Linda_Fabiani" title="Linda Fabiani">Linda Fabiani</a>, the Minister for Europe of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>, during the late summer of 2007, the 90th anniversary of the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESG2007_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESG2007-185">[178]</a></sup> In July 2017 a two-day event was organised in Ypres to mark the centenary of the battle. Members of the <a href="/wiki/British_royal_family" title="British royal family">British Royal family</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime-Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a> joined the ceremonies, which started in the evening of 30 July with the service at Menin Gate, followed by ceremonies at the Market Square. On the following day, a ceremony was held at Tyne Cot cemetery, headed by the <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[180]</a></sup></p>
